machine learning (pytorch, tensorflow)\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eGenerally, people say to avoid python for writing efficient programs. Among other things, this is because Python is \"interpreted\".\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e{: .note }\nThere are alternative implemenations of Python that aren't interpreted, but are rather compiled!\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eBut, people often use it in machine learning, which is a compute-intensive application. What gives? Well, Python can use something called a \"C extension\" - calling code written in other higher-performance languages like C and C++. dir=\"auto\"\u003ePause. What does this tell us about Python?\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003col dir=\"auto\"\u003e\n\u003cli\u003ethe Python interpreter runs from top-down\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003edefining a function with \u003ccode\u003edef\u003c/code\u003e does not run it\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eeven if we have a function called \u003ccode\u003emain()\u003c/code\u003e, \u003cstrong\u003eit won't always be run\u003c/strong\u003e!\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ol\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eSo, how would we define a main function? We will use a \"magic\" piece of code like this:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" dir=\"auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"if __name__ == \u0026quot;__main__\u0026quot;:\n  main()\"\u003e\u003cpre\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003eif\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003e__name__\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003e==\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-s\"\u003e\"__main__\"\u003c/span\u003e:\n  \u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003emain\u003c/span\u003e()\u003c/pre\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eWhen we run a python script from the command line, the interpreter will set \u003ccode\u003e__name__\u003c/code\u003e to \u003ccode\u003e\"__main__\"\u003c/code\u003e. It's a \"special variable\"!\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eWhy not just do a top-level call to \u003ccode\u003emain()\u003c/code\u003e, like this?\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" dir=\"auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"main()\"\u003e\u003cpre\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003emain\u003c/span\u003e()\u003c/pre\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eWhile it would fix our immediate problem, it would \u003cem\u003ealso\u003c/em\u003e run the code when we imported the module - which is not what we want!\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"markdown-heading\" dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003ch3 tabindex=\"-1\" class=\"heading-element\" dir=\"auto\"\u003eMore Variables and Types\u003c/h3\u003e\u003ca id=\"user-content-more-variables-and-types\" class=\"anchor\" aria-label=\"Permalink: More Variables and Types\" dir=\"auto\"\u003eConsider the following code: will it generate the error:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul dir=\"auto\"\u003e\n\u003cli\u003ebefore the program runs\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eon line #1\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eon line #2\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" dir=\"auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"def add_n_print(x,y):\n  print(x + y) # Line 1\n\ndef main():\n  add_n_print('foo', 5) # Line 2\"\u003e\u003cpre\u003e\u003cspan We'd get an error of the form:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"snippet-clipboard-content notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"TypeError: can only concatenate str (not \u0026quot;int\u0026quot;) to str\"\u003e\u003cpre class=\"notranslate\"\u003e\u003ccode\u003eTypeError: can only concatenate str (not \"int\") to str\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eUnlike C++, Python's type-checker is at \u003cem\u003eruntime\u003c/em\u003e. The type-checker detects that the types of values that \u003ccode\u003ea\u003c/code\u003e and \u003ccode\u003eb\u003c/code\u003e refer to are incompatible, and generates an error.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul dir=\"auto\"\u003e\n\u003cli\u003ethis is called \u003cstrong\u003edynamic typing\u003c/strong\u003e - which we'll talk about in-depth\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003ehowever, note that Python \u003cem\u003estill has types\u003c/em\u003e!\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e(and, this makes things harder to debug :/)\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e{: .note }\nFun fact: Python has recently supported \"type hints\", which add Haskell-like type annotations to functions. These \u003cem\u003earen't\u003c/em\u003e enforced by the interpreter!\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"markdown-heading\" dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003ch3 tabindex=\"-1\" class=\"heading-element\" Python's \u003ccode\u003erange()\u003c/code\u003e behaves \u003cem\u003eslightly\u003c/em\u003e differently from Haskell's ranges:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul dir=\"auto\"\u003e\n\u003cli\u003ePython's \u003ccode\u003erange()\u003c/code\u003e is semi-open: it includes the first item, but not the last. dir=\"auto\"\u003eAnswers:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003col dir=\"auto\"\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eUnlike C++, Python requires an explicit \u003ccode\u003eself.\u003c/code\u003e to identify member variables.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eAgain, unlike C++, Python requires an explicit \u003ccode\u003eself.\u003c/code\u003e to identify member functions.\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003ePython's access modifiers are built into variable names: two underscores creates a private method (compare \u003ccode\u003e__update_odometer\u003c/code\u003e to \u003ccode\u003eget_odometer\u003c/code\u003e). Other variables are public by default!\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ol\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e{: .note }\nSyntactically, the definition of a Python class is a sequence of statements that gets run anytime the class is instantiated. So, you can include any legal Python code in your class definition!\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"markdown-heading\" dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003ch3 tabindex=\"-1\" class=\"heading-element\" dir=\"auto\"\u003eObject Allocation\u003c/h3\u003e\u003ca id=\"user-content-object-allocation\" class=\"anchor\" aria-label=\"Permalink: Object Allocation\" href=\"#object-allocation\"\u003e\u003csvg class=\"octicon octicon-link\" viewBox=\"0 0 16 16\" version=\"1.1\" width=\"16\" height=\"16\" aria-hidden=\"true\"\u003e\u003cpath d=\"m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z\"\u003e\u003c/path\u003e\u003c/svg\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThis question has a bit of setup.   { this-\u0026gt;rad = rad; }\n\nprivate:\n  double rad;\n};\"\u003e\u003cpre\u003e#\u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003einclude\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-s\"\u003e\u003cspan dir=\"auto\"\u003e...\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eAnswer: Python uses something \u003cem\u003elike\u003c/em\u003e pointers, called an \u003cstrong\u003eobject reference\u003c/strong\u003e. Every Python object is allocated on the heap. Unlike C++, we don't use any special syntax to \"dereference\" a pointer.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e{: .note }\nIn Python, \u003cem\u003eall\u003c/em\u003e variables are object references. This applies to primitives in other languages, like numbers -- \u003ccode\u003e42\u003c/code\u003e. On reassignment, we don't \"change\" the allocated \u003ccode\u003e42\u003c/code\u003e; instead, we allocate a new number, and point to it. Though, this is mostly abstracted away from you as a programmer!\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eThis affects how we call methods too:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"highlight dir=\"auto\"\u003eAnswer:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"snippet-clipboard-content notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"1 1\n1 2\n2 2\"\u003e\u003cpre class=\"notranslate\"\u003e\u003ccode\u003e1 1\n1 2\n2 2\n\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eWhy?\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cul dir=\"auto\"\u003e\n\u003cli\u003e\u003ccode\u003ething_count\u003c/code\u003e is a \u003cem\u003eclass\u003c/em\u003e variable\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003ewhen a class is defined, Python creates a special \"class\" object that's \u003cem\u003eshared\u003c/em\u003e by all instances of the class; it represents the \u003cem\u003eentire\u003c/em\u003e class\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003eso, we can think of \u003ccode\u003ething_count\u003c/code\u003e as being \"shared\" across different instances of \u003ccode\u003eThing\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003ewe can access class (member) variables with the class name - like \u003ccode\u003eThing.thing_count += 1\u003c/code\u003e\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003cli\u003ein contrast, \u003ccode\u003eself.value\u003c/code\u003e is an \u003cem\u003einstance\u003c/em\u003e (member) variable\u003c/li\u003e\n\u003c/ul\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"markdown-heading\" dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003ch3 tabindex=\"-1\" class=\"heading-element\" dir=\"auto\"\u003eAnswer: prints \u003ccode\u003e0\u003c/code\u003e! This is a consequence of Python's ojbect reference behaviour. Assignment does not copy by default!\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eBut, we can implement our own copy implementation. One way to do this is with the \u003ccode\u003ecopy.deepcopy()\u003c/code\u003e function:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto\" dir=\"auto\" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content=\"import copy\n\nc = Circle(10)\nprint(c.area())\n\nc2 = copy.deepcopy(c)\nc.set_radius(0)\nprint(c2.area) # not 0!\"\u003e\u003cpre\u003e\u003cspan class=\"pl-k\"\u003eimport\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003ecopy\u003c/span\u003e\n\n\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003ec\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003e=\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-v\"\u003eCircle\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003e10\u003c/span\u003e)\n\u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003eprint\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003ec\u003c/span\u003e.\u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003earea\u003c/span\u003e())\n\n\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003ec2\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003e=\u003c/span\u003e \u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003ecopy\u003c/span\u003e.\u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003edeepcopy\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003ec\u003c/span\u003e)\n\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003ec\u003c/span\u003e.\u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003eset_radius\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-c1\"\u003e0\u003c/span\u003e)\n\u003cspan class=\"pl-en\"\u003eprint\u003c/span\u003e(\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003ec2\u003c/span\u003e.\u003cspan class=\"pl-s1\"\u003earea\u003c/span\u003e) \u003cspan class=\"pl-c\"\u003e# not 0!\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/pre\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e\"Deep copying\" recurisvely makes a copy of the top-level object, and \u003cem\u003eevery\u003c/em\u003e object it refers to.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eIn contrast, shallow copying - done with \u003ccode\u003ecopy.copy()\u003c/code\u003e - copies just the top level object (but not recursively).\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"markdown-heading\" dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003ch3 tabindex=\"-1\" class=\"heading-element\" dir=\"auto\"\u003eAutomatic Garbage Collection\u003c/h3\u003e\u003ca id=\"user-content-automatic-garbage-collection\" class=\"anchor\" aria-label=\"Permalink: Automatic Garbage Collection\" href=\"#automatic-garbage-collection\"\u003e\u003csvg class=\"octicon octicon-link\" viewBox=\"0 0 16 16\" version=\"1.1\" width=\"16\" height=\"16\" aria-hidden=\"true\"\u003e\u003cpath d=\"m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z\"\u003e\u003c/path\u003e\u003c/svg\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eIn Python, you don't have to worry about freeing objects when you're done with them, like you have to in C++. Instead, Python keeps track of who's pointing to each object; when nothing points to it, Python cleans it up.\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eMore broadly, this is called \"automatic memory management\".\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003e{: .note }\nWe'll cover this much more in-depth in the coming weeks!\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eIn other words: we don't need the \u003ccode\u003enew\u003c/code\u003e and \u003ccode\u003edelete\u003c/code\u003e keywords!\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"markdown-heading\" dir=\"auto\"\u003e\u003ch3 tabindex=\"-1\" class=\"heading-element\" dir=\"auto\"\u003eClasses and Destructors\u003c/h3\u003e\u003ca id=\"user-content-classes-and-destructors\" class=\"anchor\" aria-label=\"Permalink: Classes and Destructors\" href=\"#classes-and-destructors\"\u003e\u003csvg class=\"octicon octicon-link\" viewBox=\"0 0 16 16\" version=\"1.1\" width=\"16\" height=\"16\" aria-hidden=\"true\"\u003e\u003cpath d=\"m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z\"\u003e\u003c/path\u003e\u003c/svg\u003e\u003c/a\u003e\u003c/div\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003ePython has \"destructors\", but they're rarely used, and not guaranteed to run. More generally, they're really a type of \"finalizer\".\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cp dir=\"auto\"\u003eIt looks like this:\u003c/p\u003e\n\u003cdiv class=\"highlight highlight-source-python notranslate    self.book.read()\n\n  def __del__(self):\n    if self.book.finished_reading():\n      print(\u0026quot;You graduated!\u0026quot;)\"\u003e\u003cpre\u003e\u003cspan <p dir="auto">{: .note }
Hey everyone, Matt here! This lecture is <em>disjoint</em> from the last one, and focuses entirely on Python (instead of functional programming). It covers slides 1-36 of <a href="https://docs.google.com/presentation/d/1Tool2iOyv022hq5uBF8AjR091CZmu2vM/edit?usp=share_link&amp;ouid=106377371757242811146&amp;rtpof=true&amp;sd=true" rel="nofollow">Python Palooza</a>. Let me know if you've got any corrections or questions!</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h2 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Table of Contents</h2><a id="user-content-table-of-contents" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Table of Contents" href="#table-of-contents"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">{: .no_toc }</p>
<p dir="auto">{:toc}</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>dummy item</li>
</ul>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h2 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Just a Bit on Python</h2><a id="user-content-just-a-bit-on-python" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Just a Bit on Python" href="#just-a-bit-on-python"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">What do people use Python for?</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>quick and dirty scripting</li>
<li>industrial scripts</li>
<li>web backends (ex Flask and Django)</li>
<li>data science (pandas, numpy) and machine learning (pytorch, tensorflow)</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">Generally, people say to avoid python for writing efficient programs. Among other things, this is because Python is "interpreted".</p>
<p dir="auto">{: .note }
There are alternative implemenations of Python that aren't interpreted, but are rather compiled!</p>
<p dir="auto">But, people often use it in machine learning, which is a compute-intensive application. What gives? Well, Python can use something called a "C extension" - calling code written in other higher-performance languages like C and C++. This is what powers most data science and machine learning libraries like pytorch and tensorflow do under-the-hood!</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">"Pythonic"</h3><a id="user-content-pythonic" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: &quot;Pythonic&quot;" href="#pythonic"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">Compared to other languages, Python is relatively opinionated on the right way to do things. Doing things the right way is often called "Pythonic"; we'll use this word throughout these notes.</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h2 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Learning Python through Challenges - Basics</h2><a id="user-content-learning-python-through-challenges---basics" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Learning Python through Challenges - Basics" href="#learning-python-through-challenges---basics"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">Much of this lecture is learning program characteristics through examples. Let's get started!</p>
<p dir="auto">(implicitly, we're assuming some existing Python knowledge from CS35L and other prerequisites)</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Program Execution</h3><a id="user-content-program-execution" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Program Execution" href="#program-execution"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">Consider this following code:</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="# deltaco.py
def lets_go(person):
  print('Hey, ' + person)
  print(&quot;Let's go to Del Taco!&quot;)

lets_go('Carey')

def main():
  print('Oh crap, now I have gas!')

print('Nom nom')"><pre><span class="pl-c"># deltaco.py</span>
<span class="pl-k">def</span> <span class="pl-en">lets_go</span>(<span class="pl-s1">person</span>):
  <span class="pl-en">print</span>(<span class="pl-s">'Hey, '</span> <span class="pl-c1">+</span> <span class="pl-s1">person</span>)
  <span class="pl-en">print</span>(<span class="pl-s">"Let's go to Del Taco!"</span>)

<span class="pl-en">lets_go</span>(<span class="pl-s">'Carey'</span>)

<span class="pl-k">def</span> <span class="pl-en">main</span>():
  <span class="pl-en">print</span>(<span class="pl-s">'Oh crap, now I have gas!'</span>)

<span class="pl-en">print</span>(<span class="pl-s">'Nom nom'</span>)</pre></div>
<p dir="auto">When we run it, we get:</p>
<div class="snippet-clipboard-content notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="$ python3 deltaco.py
Hey, Carey
Let's go to Del Taco!
Nom nom"><pre class="notranslate"><code>$ python3 deltaco.py
Hey, Carey
Let's go to Del Taco!
Nom nom
</code></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">Pause. What does this tell us about Python?</p>
<ol dir="auto">
<li>the Python interpreter runs from top-down</li>
<li>defining a function with <code>def</code> does not run it</li>
<li>even if we have a function called <code>main()</code>, <strong>it won't always be run</strong>!</li>
</ol>
<p dir="auto">So, how would we define a main function? We will use a "magic" piece of code like this:</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="if __name__ == &quot;__main__&quot;:
  main()"><pre><span class="pl-k">if</span> <span class="pl-s1">__name__</span> <span class="pl-c1">==</span> <span class="pl-s">"__main__"</span>:
  <span class="pl-en">main</span>()</pre></div>
<p dir="auto">When we run a python script from the command line, the interpreter will set <code>__name__</code> to <code>"__main__"</code>. It's a "special variable"!</p>
<p dir="auto">Why not just do a top-level call to <code>main()</code>, like this?</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="main()"><pre><span class="pl-en">main</span>()</pre></div>
<p dir="auto">While it would fix our immediate problem, it would <em>also</em> run the code when we imported the module - which is not what we want!</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">More Variables and Types</h3><a id="user-content-more-variables-and-types" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: More Variables and Types" href="#more-variables-and-types"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">Consider the following code: will it generate the error:</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>before the program runs</li>
<li>on line #1</li>
<li>on line #2</li>
</ul>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="def add_n_print(x,y):
  print(x + y) # Line 1

def main():
  add_n_print('foo', 5) # Line 2"><pre><span class="pl-k">def</span> <span class="pl-en">add_n_print</span>(<span class="pl-s1">x</span>,<span class="pl-s1">y</span>):
  <span class="pl-en">print</span>(<span class="pl-s1">x</span> <span class="pl-c1">+</span> <span class="pl-s1">y</span>) <span class="pl-c"># Line 1</span>

<span class="pl-k">def</span> <span class="pl-en">main</span>():
  <span class="pl-en">add_n_print</span>(<span class="pl-s">'foo'</span>, <span class="pl-c1">5</span>) <span class="pl-c"># Line 2</span></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">...</p>
<p dir="auto">Answer: it generates an error when the code reaches line #1 <em>at runtime</em>. We'd get an error of the form:</p>
<div class="snippet-clipboard-content notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="TypeError: can only concatenate str (not &quot;int&quot;) to str"><pre class="notranslate"><code>TypeError: can only concatenate str (not "int") to str
</code></pre></div>
<p dir="auto">Unlike C++, Python's type-checker is at <em>runtime</em>. The type-checker detects that the types of values that <code>a</code> and <code>b</code> refer to are incompatible, and generates an error.</p>
<ul dir="auto">
<li>this is called <strong>dynamic typing</strong> - which we'll talk about in-depth</li>
<li>however, note that Python <em>still has types</em>!</li>
<li>(and, this makes things harder to debug :/)</li>
</ul>
<p dir="auto">{: .note }
Fun fact: Python has recently supported "type hints", which add Haskell-like type annotations to functions. These <em>aren't</em> enforced by the interpreter!</p>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Variables and Types</h3><a id="user-content-variables-and-types" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Variables and Types" href="#variables-and-types"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">Will this <code>trick_or_treat()</code> function generate an error, or will it work?</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="def trick_or_treat(name, age):
  if age &lt; 10:
    goodie = 'candy corn'
    print('Boooooo!')
  else:
    goodie = 'Snickers'
    print('Bwahahahahaha!')

  print(f&quot;{name}, here's your {goodie}!&quot;)

trick_or_treat('Felix', 5)"><pre><span class="pl-k">def</span> <span class="pl-en">trick_or_treat</span>(<span class="pl-s1">name</span>, <span class="pl-s1">age</span>):
  <span class="pl-k">if</span> <span class="pl-s1">age</span> <span class="pl-c1">&lt;</span> <span class="pl-c1">10</span>:
    <span class="pl-s1">goodie</span> <span class="pl-c1">=</span> <span class="pl-s">'candy corn'</span>
    <span class="pl-en">print</span>(<span class="pl-s">'Boooooo!'</span>)
  <span class="pl-k">else</span>:
    <span class="pl-s1">goodie</span> <span class="pl-c1">=</span> <span class="pl-s">'Snickers'</span>
    <span class="pl-en">print</span>(<span class="pl-s">'Bwahahahahaha!'</span>)

  <span class="pl-en">print</span>(<span class="pl-s">f"<span class="pl-s1"><span class="pl-kos">{</span><span class="pl-s1">name</span><span class="pl-kos">}</span></span>, here's your <span class="pl-s1"><span class="pl-kos">{</span><span class="pl-s1">goodie</span><span class="pl-kos">}</span></span>!"</span>)

<span class="pl-en">trick_or_treat</span>(<span class="pl-s">'Felix'</span>, <span class="pl-c1">5</span>)</pre></div>
<p dir="auto">...</p>
<p dir="auto">Answer: it does work! Python is <strong>function-scoped</strong>: when an identifier is declared, it persists until the end of the function.</p>
<p dir="auto">Contrast this to C++, where the equivalent code <em>would</em> generate an error:</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-c++ notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="void trick_or_treat(string name, int age) {
  if (age &lt; 10) {
    string goodie = &quot;candy corn&quot;;
    cout &lt;&lt; &quot;Boooooo!&quot;;
  } else {
    string goodie = &quot;Snickers&quot;;
    cout &lt;&lt; &quot;Bwahahahahaha!&quot;;
  }
  cout &lt;&lt; name &lt;&lt; &quot;, here's your &quot; &lt;&lt; goodie &lt;&lt; &quot;!\n&quot;;
}"><pre><span class="pl-k">void</span> <span class="pl-en">trick_or_treat</span>(string name, <span class="pl-k">int</span> age) {
  <span class="pl-k">if</span> (age &lt; <span class="pl-c1">10</span>) {
    string goodie = <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">"</span>candy corn<span class="pl-pds">"</span></span>;
    cout &lt;&lt; <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">"</span>Boooooo!<span class="pl-pds">"</span></span>;
  } <span class="pl-k">else</span> {
    string goodie = <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">"</span>Snickers<span class="pl-pds">"</span></span>;
    cout &lt;&lt; <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">"</span>Bwahahahahaha!<span class="pl-pds">"</span></span>;
  }
  cout &lt;&lt; name &lt;&lt; <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">"</span>, here's your <span class="pl-pds">"</span></span> &lt;&lt; goodie &lt;&lt; <span class="pl-s"><span class="pl-pds">"</span>!<span class="pl-cce">\n</span><span class="pl-pds">"</span></span>;
}</pre></div>
<div class="markdown-heading" dir="auto"><h3 tabindex="-1" class="heading-element" dir="auto">Looping with Counters</h3><a id="user-content-looping-with-counters" class="anchor" aria-label="Permalink: Looping with Counters" href="#looping-with-counters"><svg class="octicon octicon-link" viewBox="0 0 16 16" version="1.1" width="16" height="16" aria-hidden="true"><path d="m7.775 3.275 1.25-1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1 4.95 4.95l-2.5 2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1-4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1 .018-1.042.751.751 0 0 1 1.042-.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l2.5-2.5a2.002 2.002 0 0 0-2.83-2.83l-1.25 1.25a.751.751 0 0 1-1.042-.018.751.751 0 0 1-.018-1.042Zm-4.69 9.64a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 2.83 0l1.25-1.25a.751.751 0 0 1 1.042.018.751.751 0 0 1 .018 1.042l-1.25 1.25a3.5 3.5 0 1 1-4.95-4.95l2.5-2.5a3.5 3.5 0 0 1 4.95 0 .751.751 0 0 1-.018 1.042.751.751 0 0 1-1.042.018 1.998 1.998 0 0 0-2.83 0l-2.5 2.5a1.998 1.998 0 0 0 0 2.83Z"></path></svg></a></div>
<p dir="auto">What does this program print?</p>
<div class="highlight highlight-source-python notranslate position-relative overflow-auto" dir="auto" data-snippet-clipboard-copy-content="for i in range(2,5):
 print(f'{i} witches!')

for i in range(7,1,-2):
 print(f'{i} zombies!')

goblins = 100
while goblins &gt; 0:
 if goblins % 97 == 0 or goblins &lt; 62:
   break
 print('Boo!')
 goblins -= 1
print('That was scary!')"><pre><span class="pl-k">for</span> <span class="pl-s1">i</span> <span class="pl-c1">in</span> <span class="pl-en">range</span>(<span class="pl-c1">2</span>,<span class="pl-c1">5</span>):

<span class="pl-k">for</span> <span class="pl-s1">i</span> <span class="pl-c1">in</span> <span class="pl-en">range</span>(<span class="pl-c1">7</span>,<span class="pl-c1">1</span>,<span class="pl-c1">-</span><span class="pl-c1">2</span>):
